includes the ability to remotely control infected devices using a VNC server implementation</div></li></ul><p class="article-content-paragraph">This article is a deep dive into the tactics and techniques this malware strain employs to steal personal data and evade detection.</p><h2 id="malibot_overview" class="article-content-header-2">MaliBot Overview</h2><p class="article-content-paragraph">MaliBot’s command and control (C2) is in Russia and appears to use the same servers that were used to distribute the Sality malware. Many campaigns have originated from this IP since June of 2020 (see Indicators of Compromise). It is a heavily modified re-working of the SOVA malware, with different functionality, targets, C2 servers, domains and packing schemes.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">MaliBot has an fill="#E6E6E6"></path></g><defs><clipPath id="clip0_646_27469"><rect width="16" height="16" fill="white"></rect></clipPath></defs></svg></div></div><div id="custom-bullet-item-data-2f1dd885-1604-4248-a3dd-7bf76aa9bdd7" data-testid="test-id-for-custom-bullet-item-data-2f1dd885-1604-4248-a3dd-7bf76aa9bdd7" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent">Extensive logging of any successful or failed operations, phone activities (calls, SMS) and any errors</div></li></ul><h2 id="distributing_malibot" class="article-content-header-2">Distributing MaliBot</h2><p class="article-content-paragraph">Distribution of MaliBot is performed by attracting victims to fraudulent websites where they are tricked into downloading the malware, or by directly sending SMS phishing messages (smishing) to mobile phone numbers.</p><h3 id="websites" class="article-content-header-3">Websites</h3><p class="article-content-paragraph">The malware authors have so far created two campaigns– “Mining X” and “TheCryptoApp” – each of which has a website with a download link to the malware (see Campaign Screenshots in the Appendix).</p><p class="article-content-paragraph"><strong>TheCryptoApp</strong> campaign attempts to trick people into downloading their malware instead of the legitimate TheCryptoApp – a cryptocurrency tracker app with more than 1 million downloads in the Google Play Store.<a id="mark-footnote_0" href="#article-footnote_0" class="mark-footnote-link" data-tealium-text="1" data-tealium-class="footnote-ref"><sup class="mark-footnote font-bold">1</sup></a></p><p class="article-content-paragraph">For stealth and targeting purposes, the download link will direct the user to the malware APK only if the victim visits the website from an Android device, otherwise, the download link will refer to the real TheCryptoApp app in the play store (see Figure 1 and Figure 2 ).</p><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent image-wrapper flex items-center justify-center"><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" style="max-width:990px" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent Javascript that changes the download URL based on the victim’s User-Agent header." class="w-full h-full" loading="lazy" data-testid="figure-picture-image-481469f16aab116fd655bde090abf3900402a5cc-1819x1286-png-base-image"/></picture><p data-testid="text-treatment-figure-text-image-481469f16aab116fd655bde090abf3900402a5cc-1819x1286-png" class="d-attribution py-12"></p></div></div></div><p class="article-content-paragraph">The <strong>Mining X</strong> campaign is not based on any actual application in the Google Play store, but instead presents a QR code that leads to the malware APK.</p><h3 id="smishing" class="article-content-header-3">Smishing</h3><p class="article-content-paragraph">Smishing is commonly used among mobile banker-trojans because it allows the malware to spread in a fast and controllable way, and in this case, MaliBot is no different. MaliBot can send SMS messages on-demand, and once it receives a “sendsms” command containing a text to send and a phone list from the C2 server, MaliBot sends the SMS to each phone number (Figure 3).</p><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent image-wrapper flex items-center justify-center"><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" style="max-width:990px" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent img-full"><style>

</style><div id="figure-section-image-09251ec2f94b9d70d5b5b61abeda2de7108039a0-1803x812-png" data-testid="test-id-for-figure-section-image-09251ec2f94b9d70d5b5b61abeda2de7108039a0-1803x812-png" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent figure"><picture class="figure-picture" data-testid="figure-picture-image-09251ec2f94b9d70d5b5b61abeda2de7108039a0-1803x812-png-wrapper"><img src="https://cdn.studio.f5.com/images/k6fem79d/production/09251ec2f94b9d70d5b5b61abeda2de7108039a0-1803x812.png" alt="Figure 3. MaliBot code sending smishing messages to targeted phone numbers." class="w-full h-full" loading="lazy" data-testid="figure-picture-image-09251ec2f94b9d70d5b5b61abeda2de7108039a0-1803x812-png-base-image"/></picture><p data-testid="text-treatment-figure-text-image-09251ec2f94b9d70d5b5b61abeda2de7108039a0-1803x812-png" class="d-attribution py-12">Figure 3. MaliBot code sending smishing messages to targeted phone numbers.</p></div></div></div><p class="article-content-paragraph">MaliBot’s C2 IP has been used in other malware smishing campaigns since June 2020, which raises questions about how the authors of this malware are related to other campaigns (see Campaign Screenshots).</p><h2 id="how_malibot_works" class="article-content-header-2">How MaliBot Works</h2><p class="article-content-paragraph">Android ‘packers’ are becoming increasingly popular with malware developers since they allow native code to be encrypted within the mobile app making reverse engineering and analysis much more difficult. Using the Tencent packer, MaliBot unpacks itself by decrypting an encrypted Dex file from the assets and loading it in runtime using MultiDex. We have a detailed analysis on the Tencent packer in the “Dex decryption” section in our <a href="/labs/articles/flubots-authors-employ-creative-and-sophisticated-techniques-to-achieve-their-goals-in-version-50-and-beyond" class="text-info">Flubot article</a>. Please note that not all MaliBot samples are packed.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">Once loaded, MaliBot contacts the C2 server to register the infected device, then asks the victim to grant accessibility and launcher permissions. MaliBot then registers four services that perform most of the malicious operations:</p><ul class="list-none"><li class="my-5 flex custom-bullet-item"><div id="custom-bullet-item-icon-ff3f430d-2b25-4f3d-9c0d-f10b1832c31d" data-testid="test-id-for-custom-bullet-item-icon-ff3f430d-2b25-4f3d-9c0d-f10b1832c31d" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent custom-bullet-item-icon"><div class="size-16 m-auto mr-16 hexDarkIcon" data-testid="test-id-for-icon" role="img" sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent">Alarm receiver – background service watchdog to intercept calls, register boot activity, and intercept alarms.</div></li></ul><h3 id="accessibility_api_abuse" class="article-content-header-3">Accessibility API Abuse</h3><p class="article-content-paragraph">MaliBot performs most of its malicious operations by abusing Android’s Accessibility API. The Accessibility API is a powerful tool developed to encourage Android developers to build apps accessible for users with additional needs. The Accessibility API allows mobile apps to perform actions on behalf of the user, including the ability to read text from the screen, press buttons and listen for other accessibility events.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">However, these powerful functions can also allow attackers to steal sensitive information and manipulate the device to their advantage. Flubot, Sharkbot and Teabot are just a few examples of banking trojans other than MaliBot that abuse the accessibility API. This service also allows mobile malware to maintain persistence. The malware can protect itself against uninstallation and permissions removal by looking for specific text or labels on the screen and pressing the back button to prevent them.</p><h3 id="googles_2step_verification_bypass" class="article-content-header-3">Google’s 2-Step Verification Bypass</h3><p class="article-content-paragraph">Stealing credentials is often not enough to allow an attacker to successfully log in to a victim’s account. Since Google accounts are often enabled with multifactor authentication (also known as two-factor authentication, or in Google’s case, 2-step verification), a prompt will be shown on the victim’s devices if an unknown device tries to log in. The prompt will ask the victim to grant or deny the login attempt, then match a number shown on the other device. Once they have used MaliBot to capture credentials, the attackers can authenticate to Google accounts on the C2 server using those credentials, and use MaliBot to extract the MFA codes through the following steps:</p><ul class="list-none"><li class="my-5 flex custom-bullet-item"><div id="custom-bullet-item-icon-b9fd84da-7965-4772-9db0-437170c08b68" id="injects" class="article-content-header-3">Injects</h3><p class="article-content-paragraph">When MaliBot registers the device to the C2, it also sends the device’s application list, which helps the C2 determine which overlays/injections to provide whenever an “injectlist” command is sent (Figure 10). The response from the server is a list of apps and their associated injection link (Figure 11). Each injection link contains an HTML overlay that looks identical to the original app. Figure 12-Figure 15 show example injection overlays from financial institutions, in these cases based in Spain and Italy.</p><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent image-wrapper flex items-center justify-center"><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" style="max-width:990px" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent img-full"><style>

MaliBot setting up a WebView to perform the injection.</p></div></div></div><h3 id="stealing_cookies_mfa_codes_and_wallets" class="article-content-header-3">Stealing: Cookies, MFA Codes, and Wallets</h3><p class="article-content-paragraph">MaliBot’s primary goal is the theft of personal data, credentials and financial information. It has a number of methods to accomplish this, including the ability to steal cookies, multifactor authentication (MFA) codes and crypto wallets.</p><h4 id="cookies" class="article-content-header-4">Cookies</h4><p class="article-content-paragraph">MaliBot can steal credentials and cookies of the victim’s Google account. When the victim opens a Google app from the list below, MaliBot opens a WebView to a Google sign-in page (Figure 19). The victim is forced to sign in, as they cannot use the back button to exit the WebView.</p><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent image-wrapper flex items-center justify-center"><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" style="max-width:990px" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent img-full"><style>

xl:bg-transparent">https://myaccount.google[.]com – Successful login page</div></li></ul><p class="article-content-paragraph">MaliBot extracts the email and password entered to the WebView sign-in page using the Accessibility API. Before sending the credentials to the C2 server (Figure 20), MaliBot creates redirections within the WebView to <a href="https://mail.google.com/" class="text-info">Gmail</a>, <a href="https://pay.google.com/" class="text-info">Google Pay</a>, and <a href="https://passwords.google.com/" class="text-info">Google Passwords</a> and tries to grab the cookies of each redirection. MaliBot also uses the Accessibility API to try to capture passwords from Google Passwords.</p><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent image-wrapper flex items-center justify-center"><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" style="max-width:990px" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent img-full"><style>

MaliBot exfiltrating SMS messages to C2.</p></div></div></div><h4 id="crypto_wallets" class="article-content-header-4">Crypto Wallets</h4><p class="article-content-paragraph">MaliBot is also able to steal information from “Binance” and “Trust,” which are both well-known crypto-currency wallets.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">Binance</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">MaliBot tries to retrieve the Total Balance from victim’s Binance wallet. To get to the Total Balance window, the malware uses the Accessibility Service to click through the app (Figure 28).</p><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent image-wrapper flex items-center justify-center"><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" style="max-width:990px" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent img-full"><style>

MaliBot clicking through the Binance app using the Accessibility API to find the total balance." class="w-full h-full" loading="lazy" data-testid="figure-picture-image-c356d846c4eda4211fc1ad7f63284024ec6a7dc7-4192x245-png-base-image"/></picture><p data-testid="text-treatment-figure-text-image-c356d846c4eda4211fc1ad7f63284024ec6a7dc7-4192x245-png" class="d-attribution py-12">Figure 28. MaliBot clicking through the Binance app using the Accessibility API to find the total balance.</p></div></div></div><p class="article-content-paragraph">Once the Total Balance is found (Figure 29), it then sends it to the C2 (Figure 30). Example of Seed Phrases in the Trust app.</p></div></div></div><p class="article-content-paragraph">Similarly to how MaliBot locates the Total Balance in Binance, it clicks through the app using the Accessibility API until it gets to the relevant window. Once it locates them, it extracts them and removes the digits from the sides of each phrase (Figure 32) before exfiltrating the currency and Seed Phrases (Figure 33).</p><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent image-wrapper flex items-center justify-center"><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" style="max-width:990px" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent img-full"><style>

Exfiltration of balance and seed phrases to the C2 server.</p></div></div></div><h3 id="full_remote_control_of_infected_device" class="article-content-header-3">Full Remote Control of Infected Device</h3><p class="article-content-paragraph">The Accessibility API of Android allows MaliBot to perform inputs as though it was the victim. It abuses this functionality to implement something akin to a VNC server which allows remote control of the victim’s device. The attacker is able to obtain screen captures from the victim and send input commands to the malware to perform actions. The remote control communicates with a hardcoded IP over HTTP port 1080 (see Indicators of Compromise) and offers several commands (Figure 34):</p><ul class="list-none"><li class="my-5 flex Command handling for VNC-like functionality.</p></div></div></div><p class="article-content-paragraph">This effectively creates an Accessibility API-based remote access trojan (RAT) that allows the attacker to conveniently access the device remotely.</p><h3 id="sms_sending" class="article-content-header-3">SMS Sending</h3><p class="article-content-paragraph">MaliBot can send SMS messages on-demand, which is mostly used for smishing campaigns, but can also be used for monetization by sending a Premium SMS which bills the victim’s mobile credits (if enabled). The malware verifies that it has SEND_SMS permission (Figure 35), waits for the “sendsms” command to be dispatched from the C2 (Figure 36), along with a phone number list and a text to send, and then sends the text to every phone number in the list (Figure 37).</p><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent image-wrapper flex items-center justify-center"><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" style="max-width:990px" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent img-full"><style>

MaliBot sending SMS messages to every phone number on the list provided by the C2.</p></div></div></div><h3 id="logging" class="article-content-header-3">Logging</h3><p class="article-content-paragraph">MaliBot logs any uncaught exceptions, this helps the malware authors to find and fix bugs in their code (Figure 38).</p><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent image-wrapper flex items-center justify-center"><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" style="max-width:990px" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent img-full"><style>

Error logging and reporting in the malware.</p></div></div></div><h2 id="evasion_and_stealth" class="article-content-header-2">Evasion and Stealth</h2><p class="article-content-paragraph">Malware authors commonly employ tactics to prevent victims from discovering malicious apps, and to make it harder for security researchers to uncover their true purposes. MaliBot currently uses some of these capabilities and appears to have the ability to use more in the future.</p><h3 id="current_malibot_evasion_techniques" class="article-content-header-3">Current MaliBot Evasion Techniques</h3><p class="article-content-paragraph">Android is able to pause or kill a running service in the background if it’s not active or if the OS needs the resources. To keep the Background Service of the malware alive, MaliBot sets itself as a launcher. Every time the launcher activity is visited (which is very often) it starts or wakes up the service (Figure 39).</p><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent image-wrapper flex items-center justify-center"><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" style="max-width:990px" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent img-full"><style>
.image-wrapper .pull-left {
  float: left;
  padding-right: 1.5rem;
}

.image-wrapper .pull-right {
  float: right;
  padding-left: 1.5rem;
}

.image-wrapper .one-third {
  width: 33.33333%;
}

.image-wrapper .half {
  width: 50%;
}

.image-wrapper .two-third {
  width: 66.66667%;
}

.image-wrapper::after {
  clear: both;
  content: "";
  display: table;
}
</style><div id="figure-section-image-2307087aec66d1ff618bc701c2175db608470d0e-1710x500-png" data-testid="test-id-for-figure-section-image-2307087aec66d1ff618bc701c2175db608470d0e-1710x500-png" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent figure"><picture class="figure-picture" data-testid="figure-picture-image-2307087aec66d1ff618bc701c2175db608470d0e-1710x500-png-wrapper"><img src="https://cdn.studio.f5.com/images/k6fem79d/production/2307087aec66d1ff618bc701c2175db608470d0e-1710x500.png" alt="Figure 39. MaliBot maintaining its Background Service by observing launcher activity." class="w-full h-full" loading="lazy" data-testid="figure-picture-image-2307087aec66d1ff618bc701c2175db608470d0e-1710x500-png-base-image"/></picture><p data-testid="text-treatment-figure-text-image-2307087aec66d1ff618bc701c2175db608470d0e-1710x500-png" class="d-attribution py-12">Figure 39. MaliBot maintaining its Background Service by observing launcher activity.</p></div></div></div><p class="article-content-paragraph">This capability also allows the malware to get notified on every launched application and check whether an overlay/injection attack should be performed, according to the list of overlay-ready applications provided by the C2.</p><h3 id="future_evasion_capabilities" class="article-content-header-3">Future Evasion Capabilities</h3><p class="article-content-paragraph">The malware authors included functions in MaliBot that are not used in current version. Those functions could potentially be used in later versions, however, it is also possible that the malware authors copied code from somewhere else and not used the entire functionality.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">One example is a function that can detect if its running in an emulated environment. Another example is a function responsible for setting the malware as a hidden app, so it won’t be visible in the app drawer. Both functions were seen and used in SOVA malware code.</p><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent image-wrapper flex items-center justify-center"><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" style="max-width:990px" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent img-full"><style>

  width: 33.33333%;
}

  width: 50%;

  clear: both;
  content: "";
  display: table;
}
</style><div id="figure-section-image-15d144f59c356ca31ddff34c7f24e557def35b14-2459x2280-png" data-testid="test-id-for-figure-section-image-15d144f59c356ca31ddff34c7f24e557def35b14-2459x2280-png" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent figure"><picture class="figure-picture" data-testid="figure-picture-image-15d144f59c356ca31ddff34c7f24e557def35b14-2459x2280-png-wrapper"><img src="https://cdn.studio.f5.com/images/k6fem79d/production/15d144f59c356ca31ddff34c7f24e557def35b14-2459x2280.png" alt="Figure 40. Unused capability in MaliBot that checks various device attributes against hardcoded values to determine whether the device is running in an emulated environment." class="w-full h-full" loading="lazy" data-testid="figure-picture-image-15d144f59c356ca31ddff34c7f24e557def35b14-2459x2280-png-base-image"/></picture><p data-testid="text-treatment-figure-text-image-15d144f59c356ca31ddff34c7f24e557def35b14-2459x2280-png" class="d-attribution py-12">Figure 40. Unused capability in MaliBot that checks various device attributes against hardcoded values to determine whether the device is running in an emulated environment.</p></div></div></div><h2 id="conclusion" class="article-content-header-2">Conclusion</h2><p class="article-content-paragraph">MaliBot is most obviously a threat to customers of Spanish and Italian banks, but we can expect a broader range of targets to be added to the app as time goes on. In addition, the versatility of the malware and the control it gives attackers over the device mean that it could, in principle, be used for a wider range of attacks than stealing credentials and cryptocurrency. In fact, any application which makes use of WebView is liable to having the users’ credentials and cookies stolen.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">The F5 Labs <a href="/labs/articles/2022-application-protection-report-in-expectation-of-exfiltration" class="text-info">2022 Application Protection Report</a> also noted that while the rise of ransomware has been the most dramatic attacker trend in the last two years, 2021 also saw a more subtle rise in malware infections that exfiltrated data without pursuing encryption and a ransom. Such a capable and versatile example of mobile malware serves as a reminder that the attack trends <em>du jour</em> are never the only threat worth paying attention to. We hope the following indicators of compromise are helpful for responders in mitigating this threat.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph"><strong>Think we got something wrong? Have questions for the authors? Comments are welcome below!</strong></p><h2 id="appendix" class="article-content-header-2">Appendix</h2><h3 id="campaign_screenshots" class="article-content-header-3">Campaign Screenshots</h3><p class="article-content-paragraph">Additional screenshots were captured during the investigation of MaliBot which show previous attacker campaigns (Figure 41) and malicious websites used to trick victims in to downloading the malicious Android app (Figure 42, Figure 43, and Figure 44).</p></div></div><div id="article-actions-section" data-testid="testid-for-article-actions-section" class="grid Protection is the range of security options that defenders use to protect apps and their related data from a range of threats. This includes process-based practices such as securing applications during development (DevSecOps), access controls, code obfuscation, and patch management. It also includes active protection in the form of encryption, anomaly detection, and firewalls. The goal is to prevent unauthorized access, data breaches, tampering, or exploitation of vulnerabilities that could compromise the integrity and functionality of the applications.\n\nThis stems from a recognition that apps are increasingly the target of choice for malicious actors, for much the same reason that criminals rob banks: that's \"where the money is\". Given that, it increasingly makes sense for defenders to be app-centric in their protection models and mitigation strategies. Providing robust application security ensures the safety of sensitive information, builds customer trust, ensures regulatory compliance, and minimizes financial and reputational damages.20:T548,Threat actors target applications by exploiting vulnerabilities and misconfigurations. Common attack methods include injecting malicious code through techniques like SQL injection, cross-site scripting (XSS), and cross-site request forgery (CSRF), as well as abusing APIs to access sensitive data. They also exploit insec"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"ure authentication by using stolen credentials (via credential stuffing) or brute force attacks, and conduct malware injections, supply chain attacks, and reverse engineering to compromise the application or its users. Threat actors may also take advantage of misconfigured environments, such as exposed admin dashboards, unencrypted data, or default credentials. Denial-of-service (DoS) attacks can overwhelm applications and degrade their availability, while zero-day exploits leverage undiscovered vulnerabilities before they are patched.\n\nIn addition to technical attacks, threat actors use social engineering tactics, such as phishing, to manipulate users into revealing credentials or performing unauthorized actions. They may also target client-side vulnerabilities, such as intercepting unencrypted traffic or injecting malicious scripts into websites via formjacking. These attacks are often automated using tools to scan for application weaknesses or abuse APIs, but can also involve highly targeted, manual exploitation."])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"19:[\"$\",\"footer\",null,{\"children\":[[\"$\",\"div\",null,{\"id\":\"footer-credibility-section\",\"data-testid\":\"test-id-for-footer-credibility-section\",\"style\":{},\"className\":\"f5-section Begin by prioritizing security: implement secure development practices and use robust security tools. Secure coding is essential to minimize vulnerabilities like SQL injection, cross-site scripting (XSS), and other common threats. Beneficial bots support search engines, fulfill genuine business needs, and automate repetitive tasks. While some are outright malicious and enable fraud, many other bots operate somewhere in the middle—in the murky grey area between illegal and immoral. They ignore website terms of use, prevent customers from purchasing goods and services, and can even weaken security. Existing research often examines uncontrolled bot traffic across the entire internet, but this report takes a different approach and, instead, focuses on how automated traffic changes after bot mitigation is put in place.\"}],\"markDefs\":[],\"style\":\"normal\"},{\"_key\":\"39521a04bb40\",\"_type\":\"block\",\"children\":[{\"_key\":\"76f2ee6c40ac\",\"_type\":\"span\",\"marks\":[],\"text\":\"We provide a detailed analysis of bot types, their objectives, and industry-specific impacts. A key focus is on advanced and persistent bots—those that adapt as security measures evolve. Malicious actors, including nation-states and cybercriminals, frequently modify tactics to bypass defenses, increasing attack sophistication or moving to softer targets. Bots aren’t inherently bad and can be either malicious or benign. \"},{\"_key\":\"4a0e6bde0fb7\",\"_type\":\"span\",\"marks\":[\"strong\",\"e8300b546fbe\"],\"text\":\"Benign bots\"},{\"_key\":\"15e73300442b\",\"_type\":\"span\",\"marks\":[],\"text\":\" provide basic but useful services, such as passively crawling the Web to index content on the web for search engines, or chatting with website visitors to help them find resources they need. A group of bots that are all employed by a single threat actor or for a single purpose is often referred to at a botnet.\"}],\"markDefs\":[],\"style\":\"normal\"}],\"designHint\":\"card-right\"},{\"_key\":\"7975c73fd2c5\",\"_type\":\"labsTextAndArticleCardsBlock\",\"articles\":[{\"_key\":\"e71e5b016ebb\",\"_ref\":\"labs-article-import-0e3426c3-3342-4ddb-8505-7e1124f729b8\",\"_type\":\"reference\"},{\"_key\":\"142863c5cfeb\",\"_ref\":\"labs-article-import-6dbfa298-cece-4aa7-89ba-b61dbb72dfda\",\"_type\":\"reference\"}],\"content\":[{\"_key\":\"9eb06d856a90\",\"_type\":\"block\",\"children\":[{\"_key\":\"5a7fca011b55\",\"_type\":\"span\",\"marks\":[],\"text\":\"How Do Threat Actors Use Bots for Cyberattacks?\"}],\"markDefs\":[],\"style\":\"h2\"},{\"_key\":\"bc01e679f8c7\",\"_type\":\"block\",\"children\":[{\"_key\":\"90c132c8c841\",\"_type\":\"span\",\"marks\":[],\"text\":\"Threat actors use bots for a wide range of cyberattacks—in short, anything where simple, repeatable actions on the Internet can be repeated. F5 Labs often refers to these kinds of attacks as ‘automated attacks’ to reflect the diversity of motivations and impacts associated with these methods.\"}],\"markDefs\":[],\"style\":\"normal\"}],\"designHint\":\"card-left\"},{\"_key\":\"91c1bf85696e\",\"_type\":\"labsTextAndArticleCardsBlock\",\"articles\":[{\"_key\":\"0325e91f6bcb\",\"_ref\":\"labs-article-import-5859aa1e-572f-4ec2-bed0-f09f5e6d4d76\",\"_type\":\"reference\"}],\"content\":[{\"_key\":\"26529a43396f\",\"_type\":\"block\",\"children\":[{\"_key\":\"d01aaddbd4df\",\"_type\":\"span\",\"marks\":[],\"text\":\"How Can You Stop Malicious Bots and Automated Attacks?\"}],\"markDefs\":[],\"style\":\"h2\"},{\"_key\":\"3b9521b54db3\",\"_type\":\"block\",\"children\":[{\"_key\":\"a0540bbe9886\",\"_type\":\"span\",\"marks\":[],\"text\":\"Because malicious bots are designed to imitate how humans act, the first step in bot mitigation is bot identification. Furthermore, over time threat actors have found reliable methods for \"},{\"_key\":\"27bd34cf86f4\",\"_type\":\"span\",\"marks\":[\"strong\",\"eaa0d7e19ecd\"],\"text\":\"bypassing CAPTCHA\"},{\"_key\":\"230089387600\",\"_type\":\"span\",\"marks\":[],\"text\":\", so even if this degree of user friction is acceptable, it is no longer effective.\"}],\"markDefs\":[{\"_key\":\"eaa0d7e19ecd\",\"_type\":\"internalLink\",\"reference\":{\"_ref\":\"labs-article-import-5859aa1e-572f-4ec2-bed0-f09f5e6d4d76\",\"_type\":\"reference\"}}],\"style\":\"normal\"},{\"_key\":\"65c72cac73a0\",\"_type\":\"block\",\"children\":[{\"_key\":\"cd41a26d8fc6\",\"_type\":\"span\",\"marks\":[],\"text\":\"As attackers adapt to older bot mitigation strategies, organizations are increasingly turning to tools that detect bot traffic based on a combination of behavioral and technical signals.
